Notation
-A AA={aij}: Matrices are denoted capital letters, and their corresponding elements
by the corresponding unbold, lower case letter.
-x xx: Vectors are denoted by lower case bold letters, and are their elements by the
corresponding unbold letters,x j. All vectors with roman letters are column vectors,
while all vectors with greek letters are row vectors.
-ŌĆó: Schur or Hadamard matrix product, which is an entryŌĆōwise matrix product:
AAAŌĆóBBB={aijbij}.
-Ōłå ŌłåŌłå(xxx): Diagonal matrix which has the vectorxxxas diagonal.
-ŌŖŚ: Kronecker product between arbitrary matrices,AAAŌŖŚBBB={aijBBB}.
-ŌŖĢ: Kronecker sum for square matrices,AAAŌŖĢBBB=AAAŌŖŚIII+IIIŌŖŚBBB.
-A AAŌĆ▓: Matrix or vector transpose.
-e ee: the vector of ones,eee= (1,1,...,1)ŌĆ▓, of adequate dimension.
-A AA(x) ={aij(x)}: A matrix function, i.e. a matrix of functionsaij(x).
- ŌłÅt
s(III+AAA(x)dx: Product integral of a matrix function.
-p ij(s,t): transition probabilities, p. 22.
-P PP(s,t): Transition (probability) matrix,PPP(s,t) ={pij(s,t)}.
-P i(┬Ę): Conditional distribution givenX0 =i, i.e.P i(A) =P(A|X0 =i).
-b i(t): payment rates.
-b ij(t): Lump sum payments upon transition.
-B BB(t): Matrix of lump sum payments.
-b bb(t): Vector containing the payment rates.
-╬ø ╬ø╬ø(t): Intensity matrix (function) of a timeŌĆōinhomogeneous Markov process.
-╬ø ╬ø╬ø 1(t): Transition rates which cause lump sum payments.
-╬ø ╬ø╬ø 0(t): Transition rates causing no lump sum payments.
-R RR(t): Reward matrix,RRR(t) = ╬ø╬ø╬ø(t)ŌĆóBBB(t) + ŌłåŌłåŌłå(bbb(t)).
-C CC(k)(t): Matrices containing expected values of powers of payments.
-m (k)
ij (s,t): Partial moments.
-m mm(k)(s,t): Partial moment matrix.
-m mm(k)
r (s,t): Matrix of reduced moments.
-v ij(s,t): Discounted partial moments.
-V VV(s,t): Matrix of discounted partial moments.
-V VV(t): Matrix of (partial, stateŌĆōwise prospective) reserves.
-RS ij(t): Risk sums.
-╬┤ ij: Kronecker delta, equals 1 ifi=jand zero otherwise.
-P(XŌłłs): Short notation for the density function ofXevaluated ins.

CHAPTER 1
Product integration
Many functions and objects in mathematics are symbols for something that cannot be com-
puted explicitly. Take, for example, the exponential function,f(x) = exp(╬╗x),╬╗ŌłłR, which
can be defined as the unique solution to the differential equation,
fŌĆ▓(x) =╬╗f(x), f(0) = 1.
The exponential function appears in almost any branch of mathematics, and it has a lot of
nice properties. Therefore it deserves its own symbol, usually exp(x) or e x, which can then
be used as building blocks in further derivations and expressions in mathematics.
More generally, we could consider the functionfwith solution
fŌĆ▓(x) =╬╗(x)f(x), f(0) = 1.(1)
This is a linear differential equation with a variable coefficient. The solution is given by
f(x) = exp
(Ōł½ x
0
╬╗(u)du
)
.
Hence it can be expressed by the former exponential function and an integral of the variable
coefficients.
In the following we will extend the latter to linear systems of ordinary differential equations
(ODE) with variable coefficients,
YYYŌĆ▓(t) =YYY(t)AAA(t),YYY(0) =III,(2)
whereYYY(t) is ap├Śpmatrix function,AAA(t) is ap├Śpmatrix coefficient andIIIdenotes the
identity matrix (of adequate order, herep├Śp). This is just a matrix version of (1).
The unique solution, which has a number of nice properties, can be used extensively in
the theory of timeŌĆōinhomogeneous Markov processes for finding transition probabilities (Ko-
mogorov) and in applications towards lifeŌĆōinsurance for computing reserves (Thiele) and
higher order moments of future payments (Hattendorff). Hence this solution derserves a
symbol, called the product integral.
Most texts on multistate Markov models in life insurance deal with the system of differential
equations directly instead of collecting them into matrix form and without using their generic
solution (the product integral). The use of the product integral, however, allows for a much
simplified notion and more transparent derivations.

3. NUMERICAL COMPUTATION OF PRODUCT INTEGRALS 19
3. Numerical computation of product integrals
Computing product integrals numerically does usually not cause in problems. We may
identify the product integral of a matrix functionAAA(x),
YYY(t) =
tŌłÅ
s
(III+AAA(x)dx),
as the solution to (3).
If we letx 0 =s<x 1<x 2<┬Ę┬Ę┬Ę<xN =twith
xj+1 =xj + Ōłå,Ōłå = tŌłÆs
N
then we could approximate
YYYŌĆ▓(x)Ōēł1
Ōłå
(
YYY(x+ Ōłå)ŌłÆYYY(x)
)
or
YYY(x+ Ōłå)ŌēłYYY(x) + ŌłåYYYŌĆ▓(x).
But
YYYŌĆ▓(x) =YYY(x)AAA(x)
so
YYY(x+ Ōłå)ŌēłYYY(x) +YYY(x)AAA(x)Ōłå =YYY(x)(III+AAA(x)Ōłå).
This formula also explains the idea behind the notation of the product integral.
By iterating the above formula, starting withY YY(s) =YYY(x 0) =III, we can then obtain an
approximation forYYY(t). This way of solving differential equations numerically is referred to
as Eulers method. The Euler method is not very precise, and requires a very fine grid.
In turn, the following algorithm written in R, is a soŌĆōcalled fourth order RungeŌĆōKutta
method, and computes the solution to the system of differential equations much more pre-
cisely than Eulers method:
prodint<-function(A,s,t,n){
x0 <- s
y0 <- diag(nrow(A(s)))
h<-(t-s)/n
for(i in 1:n) {
s1 <- h * y0%*%A(x0)
s2 <- h * (y0 +s1/2)%*%A(x0 + h/2)
s3 <- h * (y0 +s2/2)%*%A(x0 + h/2)
s4 <- h * (y0 + s3)%*%A(x0 + h)
y0 <- y0 + s1/6 + s2/3 + s3/3+ s4/6
x0 <- x0 + h
}
return(y0)
}

approximation to the product integral ofAAA(x) fromstot.
CHAPTER 2
Markov jump processes
1. Markov jump processes
In this chapter we consider Markov processes in continuous time that take values in a discrete
(finite or at most countable) state space. By nature, such processes are piecewise constant,
and transitions occur via jumps. They are often referred to as Markov jump processes or
continuous-time Markov chains.
The value of the process at a jump time must be defined. It can either be the value just
before the jump or the value just after. The most common convention is to assign it the
value after the jump. This assumption makes Markov jump processes continuous from the
right (and with limits from the left), i.e., they are so-called c` adl` ag processes (Continue ` a
droite, limite ` a gauche).
Definition 2.1(Markov property):A continuous-time stochastic process{X t}tŌēź0
taking values in a countable setEis called a Markov jump process with state space
Eif for allt>s>t n>┬Ę┬Ę┬Ę>t1>0 andj,i,i n,...,i0ŌłłE, we have that
P(Xt =j|Xs =i,X tn =in,...,Xt1 =i 1,X 0 =i 0) =P(Xt =j|Xs =i).(10)
In the above definition we may think ofsas the present,ta time in the future andt 0,...,tn
as times in the history. Hence the Markov property states that the distribution of the future
given the present and the past only depends on the present. Or quivalently, the future and
the past are conditionally independent given the present. The latter follows from
P(Xt =j,X tn =in,...,Xt0 =i 0|Xs =i) = P(Xt =j,X s =i,X tn =in,...,Xt0 =i 0)
P(Xs =i)
= P(Xt =j|Xs =i,X tn =in,...,Xt0 =i 0)P(Xs =i,X tn =in,...,Xt0 =i 0)
P(Xs =i)
(10)
=P(X t =j|Xs =i) P(Xs =i,X tn =in,...,Xt0 =i 0)
P(Xs =i)
=P(Xt =j|Xs =i)P(Xtn =in,...,Xt0 =i 0|Xs =i).
An example of asample path(or realisation),tŌåÆX(t,Žē) for someŽēŌłłŌä” in the sample space,
is given in Figure 1 as the blue lines. Also the c` adl` ag is clearly indicated by empty and solid
circles at the times of transition.

2. PhaseŌĆōtype distributions
Markov models in lifeŌĆōinsurance are often illustrated by means of flow diagrams like in
Figures 1 and 2.
In these models, the ultimate state of death is an absorbing state. The time until death
from a certain state, which is the time it takes for the Markov process to reach the absorbing
state, is hence random and dependent on the current state. The distribution of this random
time is said to have a phaseŌĆōtype distribution.
Let{X(t)}tŌēź0denote the Markov process with a finite state spaceE, say, and intensity
matrices ╬ø╬ø╬ø(t) ={╬╗ij(t)}i,jŌłłE.

timeŌĆōdependent intensity matrices are then on the form

3. Markovian interest rates
The classical Markovian lifeŌĆōinsurance models provide fine examples of timeŌĆōinhomogeneous
phaseŌĆōtype distribution, describing the distribution of the time until death from different
states. In these examples, the transition rates are known with specific biometric interpreta-
tions.
We now turn to a more surprising application of phaseŌĆōtype distributions towards stochastic
interest rates, in which the transition rates do not possess a physical interpretation, but are
simply present for the sake of fitting a model adequately.
The model of interest rates is as follows. Let{X(t)}tŌēź0be a Markov jump process defined

The densityf Žä(t) then has the interpretation of being the expected present value of the
current interest rate accumulated in a small time interval aroundt, andF Žä(T) is the present
value of the total accumulated interest rate during [0,T].
The Markovian interest rates have two nice and useful properties for lifeŌĆōinsurance models:
they can be fitted to real bond data (or theoretical models), and they fit nicely into the
framework of product integral representations for reserves and higherŌĆōorder moments.
We first consider how to fit to real data or calibrate to other known models.
4. Estimation of phaseŌĆōtype distributions
First, we need to assess the nature of the interest rate data. Stochastic interest rate models
are often calibrated to bond prices at different maturities or, equivalently, to yield curves.
We will use the bond prices at different maturities as our basic observable data. Assume that
we have observedB(0,T i) at maturitiesT 1,T 2,...,TN. Then this corresponds to observing
P(Žä >Ti),i= 1,...,N, whereŽäŌł╝IPH(ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,TTT(u)). This is again equivalent to observing a
histogram with breaks atT i, bin widths ofT i+1ŌłÆTi and corresponding probability mass of
B(0,Ti)ŌłÆB(0,Ti+1).
The histogram comprises information of a hypothetical sample of realisations ofŽä, but the
individual values of theŽäŌĆÖs in the sample are unknown. Since we do not have a sample
size either, we shall consider the data to beweighted data, where the data points are the
midpoints of the bins, i.e.
Žäi = Ti +Ti+1
2 ,i= 1,...,NŌłÆ1,
with weights
wi =Ti+1ŌłÆTi,i= 1,...,N.
Hence, we need to be able to fit the weighted datay yy= (Žäi,wi),i= 1,...,NŌłÆ1. We assume
that the phaseŌĆōtype distribution is timeŌĆōhomogeneous PH(ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,TTT).
The likelihood function is then given by
L(yyy;ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,TTT) =
nŌłÅ
i=1
(
ŽĆŽĆŽĆeTTTŽäittt
)wi
or logŌĆōlikelihood
Ōäō(yyy;ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,TTT) =
nŌłæ
i=1
wi log
(
ŽĆŽĆŽĆeTTTŽäittt
)
.
There is no explicit expression for the maximum likelihood estimator. Since the number of
parameters is large, a generalŌĆōpurpose maximiser can become unstable and slow.
Alternatively, sinceŽäi are the times until absorption of an underlying Markov jump process,
the problem can also be seen as an incomplete data problem. Here, the complete data are the
full trajectories until absorption, while the incomplete data are the times until absorption.
4. ESTIMATION OF PHASEŌĆōTYPE DISTRIBUTIONS 43
There are several ways to deal with incomplete data estimation, and one of the most pop-
ular ones is the so-called EstimationŌĆōMaximisation (EM) algorithm, which turns out to be
particularly well-suited for our case.
Assume that the parameters to be estimated are╬Ė╬Ė╬Ė(= (ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,TTT) in our case), wherey yydenotes
the vector of incomplete data andX XXthe random vector corresponding to the complete
(unknown) data. LetŌäōc denote the complete data likelihood. Then the EM algorithm works
as follows.
0. initialize with some ŌĆ£arbitraryŌĆØ╬Ė╬Ė╬Ė0 and letn= 0.
1. (E-step) Calculate the function
h :╬Ė╬Ė╬ĖŌåÆE╬Ė╬Ė╬Ėn
(
Ōäōc(╬Ė╬Ė╬Ė;XXX)|YYY=yyy
)
.
2. (M-step) Let╬Ė╬Ė╬Ėn+1 := argmax╬Ė╬Ė╬Ėh(╬Ė╬Ė╬Ė).
3. n=n+1; GOTO 1.
In step 0, we initiate the algorithm by choosing any starting value╬Ė ╬Ė╬Ė0 for╬Ė╬Ė╬Ė. Under these
parameters, the conditional expectation of the complete data likelihood given our incomplete
data is calculated, and in step 2, we find the parameters╬Ė╬Ė╬Ė1 that maximise the expectation.
Replacing╬Ė╬Ė╬Ė0 by╬Ė╬Ė╬Ė1, we may then continue in this way. It can be shown that the likelihood
increases in every iteration step, which makes the algorithm convergent. It is not certain if
it converges to a global or only local maximum, so different initiations may be needed to
verify this point.
Let us analyse how this algorithm looks for phase-type data.

The fractionw i =n i/nis a weight. In a histogram, the data points are in the bins, and
the weight is the probability contribution. We may take e.g., the midpoint of a bin as a
representative for the data falling into that bin.
If we want to fit to a histogram, it can be a problem if the sum of the weights is (much) less
than 1. This can happen if the survival function we consider, or equivalently the bond price,
is not observed at very many points. If the bond price for maturity 30 years, for example,
equals 0.38, then the survival function of the corresponding phase-type distribution at the
highest observed point is 0.38, significantly larger than the other weights. This is because we
do not have observations for maturities larger than 30. In this case, it is better to consider
the largest maturity to be a right-censored data point.
(d)Censored data.

ofE(B i|Y=Žän) toE(B i|Y >Žän) and similarly for the rest. Thus we get

(e)Fitting to IPH.
It is possible to fit to an IPH(ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,TTT,╬╗) with relatively minor modifications using Theorem 3.10.
In each iteration, the data are transformed back to corresponding PH(ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,TTT) data, in which
the appropriateEandMsteps are then performed. This results in updated parameters
(ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,TTT). The function╬╗(x) =╬╗(x;╬Ė) may be parameter dependent as well, and a separate
optimisation of╬╗is performed. In the next iteration, data are no transformed according to
the updated╬╗(x;╬Ė), etc.
It can be shown that this is again an EMŌĆōalgorithm.
(f)Computation. Fortunately, we can make use of the R package ŌĆ£matrixdistŌĆØ for fitting to

5. Estimation of PH bond models
Consider bond pricesB(0,T i) available at different maturitiesT 1,T 2,...,Tn. Then according
to Theorem 3.12 we have that
B(0,Ti) =ŽĆŽĆŽĆDDD(0,Ti)eee= eŽüTiP(Žä >Ti), i= 1,2,...,n,
for someŽü>0 and whereŽäŌł╝IPH(ŽĆŽĆŽĆ,MMM(u)ŌłÆŌłåŌłåŌłå(rrr(u))ŌłÆŽüIII). ThenŽümust satisfy that
eŌłÆŽüTiB(0,Ti)Ōēż1, i= 1,2,...,n.
This can be achieved by choosing
Žü= max
iŌłł{1,...,n}
(
logB(0,T i)
Ti
)
.
In the lifeŌĆōinsurance context in Denmark, by regulation, the bond prices (discounting factors)
must be computed from discrete forward rates,f d(0,Ti), published by the Danish Financial
Supervisory Authority. Thus
B(0,Ti) =
(
1 +fd(0,Ti)
)ŌłÆTi
from which
logB(0,T i)

5. ESTIMATION OF PH BOND MODELS 49
Hence
Žü= max

(
ŌłÆlog(1 +fd(0,Ti))

=ŌłÆmin
i

Hence calibrating to dataB(0,T i),i= 1,...,ncan be done by fitting PH or IPH distributions
to eŌłÆŽüTiB(0,Ti) using an EM algorithm. The possible interest rates can either be selected by
the EM algorithm (referred to as unrestricted interest rates) or fixed to values (or functions)
of our choice (restricted interest rates).
In the former case, we obtain a maximum likelihood estimate ( ╦åŽĆŽĆŽĆ,╦åTTT(x)) for the parameters.
The estimate forMMM(x) is then readily obtained from
╦åMMM(x) = ╦åTTT(x) + ŌłåŌłåŌłå(ttt(x)).
To find the induced interest rates, we also have from Theorem 3.12 that
╦åTTT(x) = ╦åMMM(x)ŌłÆŌłåŌłåŌłå(rrr(x))ŌłÆŽüIII
so we conclude that the estimated exit ratesttt(x) must satisfy
ttt(x) =rrr(x) +Žüeee,
whereeeeis the vector of ones. Hence, the induced interest rates are given by
rrr(x) =ttt(x)ŌłÆŽüeee.
Neither the transition rates nor the interest rates are unique, but the resulting discount factor
(bond price) is invariant under different representations, which is all that matters regarding
reserving in the insurance context.
If, in turn, we decide to choose the possible range of interest ratesr i(x) ourselves, then the
EMŌĆōalgorithm is modified not to update the exit rates.
In this case, the exit rates will be fixed at
ttt(x) =rrr(x) +Žüeee
so
╦åMMM(x) = ╦åTTT(x) + ŌłåŌłåŌłå(rrr(x)) +ŽüIII.
While the parametrisation of the transition rates may not be unique, the interest rates remain
fixed.
Remark 3.16:By fixing the exit rates, the class of phaseŌĆōtype distributions may no longer
be dense. If, for example, we fixr rr(x) =reee, then only the exponential distribution with
parameterris reachable.
We now present two examples of fitting to real data and one example to a theoretical model.
The estimation is computed using the RŌĆōpackagematrixdist.
50 3. A MODEL FOR STOCHASTIC INTEREST RATES
Example 3.17(Fitting to observed bond prices with restricted interest rates):Bond prices,
B(0,T) as of 31/12/2003 (time zero) with maturitiesT= 1,2,...,30 years are available from
the Danish Financial Supervisory Authority and given by 0.9755051, 0.9434934, 0.9059545,
0.8679149, 0.8251354, 0.7857250, 0.7472528, 0.7075066, 0.6679984, 0.6286035, 0.5951316,
0.5625969, 0.5310441, 0.5005108, 0.4710280, 0.4448469, 0.4197550, 0.3958013, 0.3728296,
0.3508858, 0.3319907, 0.3140894, 0.2970098, 0.2808430, 0.2654229, 0.2508400, 0.2369349,
0.2237965, 0.2112725, 0.1994495, respectively.
This corresponds to an empirical survival distribution to which we can then fit phaseŌĆōtype
distributions of different dimensions. Regarding the discretisation, we let 0.5+i,i= 0,...,29
denote the data points with probability massB(i)ŌłÆB(i+ 1), whereB(0) = 1, and a right
censored data point at 30 with probability massB(30) = 0.1994495. Since all observed bond
prices are less than one, we haveŽü= 0, corresponding to an environment with non-negative
interest rates.
We usedp= 2,3,4,5,10 and 15 phases, with stateŌĆōwise interest rates beingr p
i =i/(10p),
i= 1,...,pfor the different dimensionsp. Underlying this choice is the assumption that the
interest rates fluctuate between 1% and 10%, and ther iŌĆÖs are obtained as the points that
divide the interval [0,0.1] intop, including the right endpoint. The vectorsr rrp = (rp
1,...,rp
p)ŌĆ▓
will serve as exit rate vectors of the phaseŌĆōtype distributions to be fitted.
In Figure 3 (left), we have plotted the phaseŌĆōtype fits to the empirical survival curve for di-
mensionsp= 2,3,4,5. At dimension 3, we obtain a decent fit and excellent fits for dimensions
4 and 5. The likelihood values for 4 cases are -3.178171, -3.16838,-3.166633, and -3.166182.
Further experimentation with dimensions 10 and 15 resulted in likelihoods of -3.165002 and
-3.164654, respectively. However, the plots of bond prices and yields are indistinguishable
from the plots corresponding to dimension 5, see Figure 4. We can also assess the quality of
the fits by plotting the estimated density function vs. the weighted data, shown in Figure
5. Again the plots for dimensionsp= 5,10,15 are almost indistinguishable. Therefore, we
conclude that dimension 4 or 5 will suffice toapproximatethe bond prices.
The estimates of the subŌĆōintensity matrixMMMŌłÆŌłåŌłåŌłå(rrr) (under a risk neutral measureQ) for
dimensionsp= 3,4,5 are given by

The assessment regarding the quality of the fits depends on their use. When computing
reserves or higher-order moments in life insurance with stochastic interest rates being inde-
pendent of the biometric process, the interest rates are merely used as a discounting factor, in
which case even a 5ŌĆōstate approximation to the Vasi╦ć cek model with a state-wise deterministic
interest rate model will provide a good fit.Ō¢Ī
CHAPTER 4
MultiŌĆōstate Markov models with Markovian interest rates
1. Markovian interest rates
Consider a Markov process{Xt}tŌēź0taking values in a stateŌĆōspaceE, which defines the
biometric states of an insured.
In this chapter, in turn, we will assign a possibly different interest rate to each state. Let
rrr(t) = (ri(t))iŌłłE
denote the vector of interest rates in the different states.
The stateŌĆōspaceEmay either be the exact same for both the interest rate process and
biometric process, or entirely different stateŌĆōspaces if, e.g. the Markovian interest rate
model of Chapter 3 is to be employed.
In that case, there is a Markov jump processX r ={Xr(t)}tŌēź0with stateŌĆōspaceE r =
{1,2,...,p}and intensity matrix ╬ø╬ø╬ør(t) ={╬╗r
ij(t)}tŌēź0, say, such that the corresponding bond
pricesB(t,T) are given as in Theorem 3.12.
Additionally, we letX b ={Xb(t)}tŌēź0denote the Markov jump process governing the transi-
tion between the biometric states with the stateŌĆōspaceE b ={1,2,...,q}and intensity matrix
╬ø╬ø╬øb(t) ={╬╗b
ij(t)}tŌēź0. Hence, the Markov jump process can be written in the form
X(t) = (Xb(t),Xr(t)) (32)
with stateŌĆōspaceE=E b├ŚEr.
The product space will be ordered lexicographically, which means that state (i,j) is identified
with statek(i,j) =i(pŌłÆ1) +j,j= 1,...,p,i= 1,...,qof the stateŌĆōspace{1,2,...,pq}and
(i,j)<( ╦£i,╦£j) iffk(i,j)<k( ╦£i,╦£j).
The processesXb andXr may or may not be independent, and the payment processes likewise
may or may not be independent ofX r. In the independent case the processesX b andX r
are defined on each their stateŌĆōspace, and the common stateŌĆōspace will be the product set
of the two. If the processes share states, with the possibility of having simultaneous jumps,
then we obtain dependency of the processes. Such a case could, e.g. be a rise in the interest
rate causing an increased intensity of jumping to surrender or freeŌĆōpolicy states.

1. Basic properties
Let{Z(t)}tŌēź0denote a stochastic process taking values inE={1,2,...,p}. Then the sample
pathstŌåÆZ(t) are piecewise constant. The time the process spends in each state before
jumping to another is called a sojourn time. The duration process{U(t)}tŌēź0related to
{Z(t)}tŌēź0is defined by
U(t) = inf{uŌēź0:Z((tŌłÆu)ŌłÆ)╠Ė=Z(t)},
i.e. the time eleapsed since the last jump into the current state, which equals the value of
Z(t), or the time since initiation, which equalst, if there has been no previous jump.
Definition 5.1:{Z(t)}tŌēź0is said to be a semiŌĆōMarkov process if{(Z(t),U(t))}tŌēź0is
a Markov process.
The stateŌĆōspace for{(Z(t),U(t))}tŌēź0isE├ŚR+, and the Markov property then amounts to
P(Z(t) =j,U(t)Ōēżv|Fs) =P(Z(t) =j,U(t)Ōēżv|Z(s),U(s)),
whereF s =Žā((Z(x),U(x)):xŌłł[0,s]).

3. The lifeŌĆōinsurance model
We consider a life insurance contract, stipulating that the insured can be in one ofpdifferent
(biometric) states at any time. This could, for example, be a simple ŌĆ£aliveŌĆōdeath model (p=
2), ŌĆ£activeŌĆōdisabledŌĆōdeathŌĆØ model or ŌĆ£activeŌĆōdisabledŌĆōlapsingŌĆōdeathŌĆØ withp= 2. There,
the original ŌĆ£aliveŌĆØŌĆōstate has been refined into subconditions, which are of importance for
differentiating between payments.
The state of the insured is assumed to follow a semiŌĆōMarkov process{Z(t)}tŌēź0with stateŌĆō
spaceE={1,2,...,p}and duration process{U(t)}uŌēź0. LetMMM(t,u) ={┬Ąij(t,u)}i,jŌłłEdenote
its intensity matrix.
Given thatZ(t) =i,U(t) =u, there is a continuous benefit payment with rateb i(t,u) during
sojourns. Negative values are premium rates. We assume the matrix is split into

It looks like a simple PDE (delayed, though) with a simple initial condition. However, the
PDE is coupled to another PDE that solves for the unknownppp(s,t,0,v) (andppp(s,t,u,v)). In
the case of Markov models, we have seen how product integration together with Van LoanŌĆÖs
formula solves both transition probabilities, reserves and higher order moments. This will
be the theme of the following section, where the product integration framework is extended
to semiŌĆōMarkov models.
4. MATRIX FORM OF THE INTEGRAL EQUATION AND ITS UNIQUE SOLUTION 83

be important later on when dealing with reserves and higherŌĆōorder moments. Therefore the
following theorem is still linked to the semiŌĆōMarkov setŌĆōup in spite of the seemingly free
choice of matrix functions.

Remark 5.18:Different choice of matricesA AA0(t,u) andAAA1(t,u) will result in different
solutions. For the applications we have in mind, the matricesAAA0(t,u) andAAA1(t,u) will arise
from a unique decomposition of a matrix
AAA(t,u) =AAA0(t,u) +AAA1(t,u),
where the former matrixAAA0(t,u) has to do with properties during sojourns whileA AA1(t,u)
has to do with properties at transitions. For example, for intensity matrices ╬ø╬ø╬ø(t,u) we have
the unique decomposition into a sum of its diagonal matrix Ōłå╬ø ╬ø╬ø(t,u) and its offŌĆōdiagonal
matrixŌłć╬ø╬ø╬ø(t,u).
In the case of a unique decomposition, will use the same notation for the product integrals,
namely
t,v
Ps,u
(III+AAA(x,w)d(x,w)) and
t,vŌłÅ
s,u
(III+AAA(x,w)d(x,w)),
whenAAA0(t,u) andAAA1(t,u) arise from a unique decomposition.

1. Random setup
The setup is identitcal to Section 1, except for the lump sum payments,b ij(t), which are
now, in general, random. Therefore they will be denoted with capital lettersB ij(t), which is
both customary in probability theory and to destinguish random variables from deterministic
functions. The rest of the notation follows that of Section 1.

5. Derivative securities
We here provide a very short and actuarial approach to option pricing. Assume that a
stockprice is given by a stochastic process{S(t)}tŌēź0, in a market with constant interest rate
r>0. A timeTthe value of the stock isS(T).
Assume that we want to make an agreement thatg(S(T)) is paid out at timeTinstead of
T. That could e.g. be withg(x) = max(xŌłÆc,0), which would then amount to an European
call option with strikec: ifS(T)<c, then we would like to buy the stock at priceS(T), in
which case the option is worthless, and ifS(T)>c, then we would prefer to buy at pricec, in
which case the differenceS(T)ŌłÆcmust be priced and paid for. If instead we are interested
in selling at timeT, the corresponding arrangement would beg(x) = max(cŌłÆx,0), which is
referred to as an European put option.
The price for such arrangements can be evaluated by

In continuous time finance, models are usually written in terms of soŌĆōcalled stochastic dif-
ferential equations (SDE). This subject goes beyond the scope of these lecture notes, never-
theless, we may provide the following intuitive introduction to the subject, which contains
most of the ideas behind the rigorous theory.
IfS t denotes the price of a financial asset or portfolio, then an SDE is on the form
dSt =┬Ą(t,St)dt+Žā(t,St)dWt.
Here┬Ą(x,y) andŽā(x,y) are determinisitic (known) functions. The former is called the drift
and the latter the volatility. The process{Wt}tŌēź0is called a Browninan motion or Wiener
process. The only thing we need to know about this process is that it has independent
increment, i.e. ifsŌēżtŌēżuthenWuŌłÆWt is independent ofW tŌłÆWs, and theW tŌłÆWsŌł╝
N(0,tŌłÆs).
The symbol
dWt =Wt+dtŌłÆWtŌł╝N(0,dt).
We use the usual convention as earlier that (dt) 2 = 0.
By definition,
E(dWt) = 0

Furthermore,

When computing prices, we replace┬Ąby the riskŌĆōfree interest rater. The argumentation
is based on a soŌĆōcalled noŌĆōarbitrage argument, but may be understood as follows. From
an actuarial point of view, it seems reasonable that expectation of the present value ofS t
should exactly be its priceS 0. This is how fair premiums are computed. But

So unless┬Ą=r, the expected present value ofS t will either be worth more thanS 0 or less
thanS 0. If it is worth more, then taking a huge loan at interest rater, could provide us
with anarbitrarilylarge gain. Similarly, if it is less, then a selling argument yields the same.
To avoid the possibility arbitrary large gains, present value of any future prices relating to
St should be prices as if the drift of the process equals the interest rate.
The argument by replacing the drift by the interest is not valid in general, but a special
feature of the BlackŌĆōScholes model. In general, one looks for a change of parameters such
that the present value is exactly that ofS 0.Ō¢Ī

6. The unit-link reserve

stock prices or investments. For example, an insurance policy may stipulate that the value

Bibliographic notes
Product integration can be consulted in [9, 7] and [12]. The particular approach taken
in these notes is from [3]. The classical theory of SDE-driven interest rate models can be
consulted in many textbooks. I may recommend [4]. The Markovian interest rate model
goes at least back to [10]. In [11], a financial theory is developed for this kind of interest
rate. The content of the current notes is taken from [2]. The development of a matrixŌĆō
approach to reserves, higher order moments, and distributions of future payouts is from [5].
TimeŌĆōinhomogeneous semiŌĆōMarkov processes was first introduced in [8]. Of more recent
papers we mention [6], which deals with cashŌĆōflows in semiŌĆōMarkov models, and [1] which
established PDEs for reserves and higher order moments (nonŌĆōmatrix version).

APPENDIX A
R code for spouse model
prodint<-function(A,s,t,n){
x0 <- s
y0 <- diag(nrow(A(s)))
h<-(t-s)/n
for(i in 1:n) {
s1 <- h * y0%*%A(x0)
s2 <- h * (y0 +s1/2)%*%A(x0 + h/2)
s3 <- h * (y0 +s2/2)%*%A(x0 + h/2)
s4 <- h * (y0 + s3)%*%A(x0 + h)
y0 <- y0 + s1/6 + s2/3 + s3/3+ s4/6
x0 <- x0 + h
}
return(y0)
}
Lambda<-function(x){
A<-matrix(0,4,4)
A[1,2]<- 0.0005+10╦å(5.728+0.038*(x+6)-10)
A[1,3]<- 0.0005+10╦å(5.88+0.038*x-10)
A[2,4]<- 0.0005+10╦å(5.88+0.038*x-10)
A[3,4]<- 0.0005+10╦å(5.728+0.038*(x+6)-10)
evec<-rep(1,4)
dsum<-as.vector(A%*%evec)
return(A-diag(dsum))
}
R<-function(x,mu){
if(x<=70){
return(diag(c(-mu,-mu,0,0)))
}
else{
return(diag(c(1.2,1.2,1.2,0)))
}
}
dR<-function(x,mu){
if(x<=70){
return(diag(c(-1,-1,0,0)))
115
116 A. R CODE FOR SPOUSE MODEL
}
else{
return(diag(c(0,0,0,0)))
}
}
reserve<-function(t,TT,Lambda,R,mu,rint,N){
dim<-nrow(Lambda(t))
A11<-function(x){
return(Lambda(x)-rint*diag(1,nrow=dim))
}
RM<-function(x){
cbind(rbind(A11(x),matrix(0,dim,dim)),rbind(R(x,mu),Lambda(x)))
}
PRM<-prodint(RM,t,TT,N)
RES<-PRM[1:dim,(dim+1):(2*dim)]
return((RES%*%rep(1,dim))[1])
}
equiv_premium<-function(t,TT,Lambda,R,dR,mu,rint,numsteps){
b=reserve(t,TT,Lambda,R,0.0,rint,numsteps)
a=reserve(t,TT,Lambda,dR,0.0,rint,numsteps)
return(-b/a)
}
equiv_premium(50,100,Lambda,R,dR,0.0,0.01,5000)
R1<-function(x,mu){
if(x<=70){
return(diag(c(-1.2,-1.2,0,0)))
}
else{
return(diag(c(mu,mu,mu,0)))
}
}
dR1<-function(x,mu){
if(x<=70){
return(diag(c(0,0,0,0)))
}
else{
return(diag(c(1,1,1,0)))
}
}
equiv_premium(50,100,Lambda,R1,dR1,0.0,0.01,5000)
APPENDIX B
A short guide to fitting Markovian interest rate models
PH fitting to weighted data works in the following way. We use the library matrixdist in R.
Once installed, this is invoked by
library(matrixdist)
In the matrixdist package one may then create an object which is of phaseŌĆōtype by the
command
ph(structure="general",dimension=dim)
This generates an random ph representation, which we may see how it looks like by
myph<-ph(structure="general",dimension=dim)
myph
Here ŌĆ£generalŌĆØ simply refers to a general phaseŌĆōtime structure, as opposed to subŌĆōstructures
like e.g. Coxian, which would be then be set by ŌĆ£coxianŌĆØ .
Fitting to data is now a simple matter. Datax 1,...,xn, which may e.g. be read from a file,
are stored in an array, say called data. Then the command ŌĆ£fitŌĆØ can be used to perform a
number of iterations in an EM algorithm, which converges towards to maximum likelihood
estimate of its parameters. The following commands reads data, defines a fiveŌĆōdimensional
phaseŌĆōtype object, and performs 1000 EMŌĆōiterations in a five dimensional phaseŌĆōtype setting
with initial parameters given by ph5:
data<-scan(file="path-to-data/data.dat")
ph5<-ph(structure="general",dimension=5)
fit5 <- fit(ph5,y=data,stepsEM=1000)
The result, ŌĆ£fit5ŌĆØ is again a phaseŌĆōtype object, which may be refitted if another e.g. 2000
iterations are needed:
fit5 <- fit(fit5,y=data,stepsEM=2000)
One may also fit to weighted data. If a data point appearsnof times, it may be defined as
a weighted data point with weightn. The weight need to be an integer value,. This acutally
allows us to fit to a histogram or theoretical distribution instead.
For a given theoretical distribution functionF, choose a finite range [a,b] and subdivide it
into small intervals [yi,yi+1],i= 1,2,...,mŌłÆ1. Letxi be the midpoint of the small intervals,
117
118 B. A SHORT GUIDE TO FITTING MARKOVIAN INTEREST RATE MODELS
xi = (yi +yi+1)/2. Then we may consider thex iŌĆÖs as weighted data with weightsF(y i+1)ŌłÆ
F(yi). In particular, the latter method applied to bond prices (which behaves like a survival
function).
Weighted data are fitted in the following way:
fit5<-fit(ph,y=data,weight=weights,stepsEM=1000)
Here ŌĆ£dataŌĆØ is an array containing the data points while ŌĆ£weightsŌĆØ is an array containing
the corresponding weights. Hence they are of the same size.
We have assume a finite interval until now for the support ofF. This may no be satisfactory
if the remaining tail probability from the lastx n onwards is still large. This is often the
case for bonds. In this case if would be better to treatb, the last interval limit, as a right
censored data point. This can be handled in the following way:
fit(ph,y=uncens,weight=wuncens,rcen=cens,rcenweight=wcens,stepsEM=1000)
Here ŌĆ£ucensŌĆØ is an array which contains thex iŌĆÖs midpoints of the intervals, ŌĆ£wuncensŌĆØ an
array of the same size containing the wights, ŌĆ£rcensŌĆØ an array (in this case of one point only)
containing the cutŌĆōoff of our support, and ŌĆ£wcensŌĆØ an array of the same size as rcens which
contains the remaning tail probability.
Once fitted, the fitted distribution can be visualized by accessing e.g the density or distri-
bution functions by the commands
dens(fit5, seq(0,10, 0.01))
cdf(fit5, seq(0,10, 0.01))
where ŌĆ£seq(0,10, 0.01)ŌĆØ defines a range over 0 to 10 with steplengths 0.01 in which the
functions are evaluated. One may e.g. plot the fitted density against a histogram using
hist(data,probability = T)
lines(seq(0,10, 0.01),dens(fit5, seq(0,10, 0.01)))
Regarding fitting to bond prices, we need to plot prices against the survival function of the
fit. Assume that the bond prices are contained in the array ŌĆ£bondŌĆØ with corresponding
maturities in ŌĆ£matŌĆØ . Then
plot(mat,bond,ylab="BondŌÉŻprice",xlab="Maturity")
lines(seq(0,300, 0.1), 1-cdf(ph10, seq(0,300, 0.1)))
plots the fitted ph distribution to bond prices in terms of its survival function. The exit rate
vector contains the estimated interest rates used in order to achieve the fit.
If one prefers to use preŌĆōspecified interest rates, specified in the vectorr rr= (r 1,...,rp), then
one can replace the exit rate vector in each step of the EM algorithm byr rrinstead. The is
achieved by replacing the intensity matrixTTTobtained in an EM step by
TTT+ ŌłåŌłåŌłå(ttt)ŌłÆŌłåŌłåŌłå(rrr),
B. A SHORT GUIDE TO FITTING MARKOVIAN INTEREST RATE MODELS 119
wheretttis the estimated exit rate vector. In R language:
for(i in 1:1000){
ph <- fit(ph,y=uncens,weight=wuncens,rcen=cens,rcenweight=wcens,stepsEM=1)
exit <- -rowSums(ph@pars$S)
ph@pars$S <- ph@pars$S + diag(exit)-diag(r)
}
}
The command ŌĆ£ph@pars$SŌĆØ pulls the intensity matrix out of the phaseŌĆōtype object ŌĆ£phŌĆØ .
